===== PAGE 12 =====
Jerome Lipnick
Rabbi’s Message
It is with extreme pleasure that I make my debut
in your year book, and I do so conscious of the import¬
ance of this initial appearance. For us this a Berashit,
the beginning of my association with this institution.
I am now part of your record, and that is no small
thing.
What a beautiful tradition you have created and
sustained in the Center, that over the years at this
season you have put together this book. You have
assembled all of the various aspects and activities of
this congregation and this community in order to view
them whole. The view that you receive through word
and through picture now becomes a slice of Jewish
history.
It takes courage to keep the record. There are
those who cynically warn against putting things in
writing for fear that the written word may return to
haunt us. The warning is not without foundation. The
fear is a valid one. But happily you have not yielded
to them. You have insisted on the record and you have
published photographs and facts and names too, the
names of each Center member and family who belong
to our common undertaking and who have a crucial
stake in what we do. Let all who wish examine the
record of what has been achieved, and let them judge
accordingly.
Year books may be new to Jewish experience, but
not books for the years. For example the Bible repre¬
sents the long history of a people whose origins were in
the Ur of The Chaldeese, which wandered into Canaan,
and then descended into Egypt to be prepared and
tested in the crucible 3f enslavement for its grand
journey over the centuries, the bearers of a heritage
of learning and justice and human decency. Wherever
we have gone in all the lands of our dispersion, in every
country in which we have lived and especially in the
land of Israel itself we have ever been conscious of
what we have achieved and what we have failed to
achieve. We have obligated ourselves to be literate in
many languages and to leave it to succeeding genera¬
tions to determine the quality of our efforts. As Jews
we have become adept in the art of dealing with His¬
tory, hoping and praying that history will deal kindly
with us.
History is rarely given to leniency even though a
Holy Day season is a time of forgivenss and trying
RABBI JEROME LIPNICK
to start anew. We ask no favors. The record must be
an accurate and honest one. Knowing this, it must
represent the very best of which we are capable as
individuals and as an assemblage of families. And year
by year the record must be scrutinized by each of us
so that we ourselves might introduce the changes that
are necessary and the approaches that are required to
make our endeavors worth while in our own eyes and
in the eyes of others.
There have been significant achievements here,
many of them unique, and for this I and all of us
have reason to be gratified. I personally stand on the
shoulders of numerous predecessors in the rabbinate
and the laity who have labored with zeal and intelli¬
gence to create this Synagogue-Center. What we have,
I believe shows the result of enormous planning and
devotion. Yet each year carries with it its own obliga¬
tions and responsibilities as we build for tomorrow,
and we as for today.
My wife and I and our children appreciate that
you have accepted us into this enterprise and that you
have welcomed us so warmly. Now that we are in your
midst may we join you, and will you join us, in the
work that must be undertaken if we are to continue
to be worthy custodians of the heritage of Israel. We
are the heirs of a great record. Let us add our volume
to its glorious story.
Rabbi Jerome Lipnick

The formation of the new translation
Torah
Students of Mrs. Carl Hackel’s Kindergarten Class
studying the Torah.
If you will look at the seal of the Jackson¬
ville Jewish Center you will see three very
important words taken from the Ethics of the
Fathers. They are Torah, Avodah, Gemilat
Hasadim; Study, Prayer and Deeds of Kind¬
ness. For over 50 years the Jacksonville Jew¬
ish Center has used this as its credo. The
guiding force assisting us in the fulfillment of
these three precepts has been the United
Synagogue of America.
To illustrate Torah, we proudly point to our
school system starting with Kindergarten and
going all the way through high school. We
have worked ever closely with the United
Synagogue Commission on Jewish education
in seeing that our school system maintains
the high level that is expected of all conserva¬
tive congregations. We point with pride to
our adult education which takes its direction
from the National Academy for Adult Jewish
Studies. The Academy, as well as the Jack¬
sonville Jewish Center has worked tirelessly
to create a climate for adult Jewish learning
and to raise standards in this important field.
In fact, second to the education of our chil¬
dren the activities of adult Jewish education
will determine the very future of our syna¬
gogue and our sister congregations through¬
out the United States. We point with pride to
our youth department ranging from the
Tween-Agers through senior United Syna¬
gogue youth groups and to the Ramah pro¬
gram that this synagogue encourages for our
youngsters. We point with pride to our ATID
group which in the past few years has emerged
as a fullfledged member of our synagogue.
These young adults have made Shabbat and
Torah something livable for our generation.
“On three things the world stands, Torah,
Prayer and Deeds of Kindness, our house of
worship is truly a house of Torah".
mop
(Prayer and Activity)
Besides meaning prayer, it means activity,
and this word is synonymous with the Jack¬
sonville Jewish Center. The Center is truly a
center of activities, ranging from congrega¬
tional standards that concern themselves with
Jewish law, the Sabbath, Kashrut, public
functions, fund raising, Bar Mitzvah stan¬
dards, and the relationship with other con¬
gregations in the general community. We have
been a beacon of the Jacksonville community
12
===== PAGE 14 =====
because our standards and activities center
around the desire to make Judaism more
meaningful to our members. We have shown
that Torah, activity and deeds of kindness
are not something that is visible only on the
seal of our Center but something that is vis¬
ible from the youngest member of our con¬
gregation to the not so young. From the
youngest child lighting the Sabbath candles
in the classroom to the not so young filling
the sanctuary on the Sabbath. From Third
and Silver Street to State Road No. 13 the
Center stretches out its hand not only in time
and space but also in deeds and thought.
“On three things the world stands, Torah,
Prayer and Deeds of Kindness, our house of
worship is truly a house of prayer and ac¬
tivity.”
□HD“ mTDJ
(Deeds of Kindness)
To translate the moral and ethical impera¬
tives of Judaism into everyday activities in
the public arena is the platform on which we
stand. In this platform is contained our feel¬
ing for social action. “Have we not all one
father? Hath not one God created us?”
The words of Malachi are the words we
say to all our fellow human beings oppressed
in all the four corners of the earth whether it
be our own American negro or our fellow
Jews in the Soviet Union for we know only
too well the consequences of bigotry and
hatred. In our time we have seen the de¬
struction and the inhumanities to our fellow
man. The Jacksonville Jewish Center con¬
fronts the major issues of the day. Whatever
they may be we see it is our task in the
light of Jewish tradition to take the stand by
means of debate or action in response to these
problems.
Also involved in theory and practice is our
awareness of the ecumenical spirit that now
prevails. We of the Jacksonville Jewish Cen¬
ter are doing our part in our community to
better understand our brethren of different de¬
nominations and to help them understand us.
Our concern for the sick, our response to
the aged, our involvement with the troubled
have portrayed to the community and our
membership the heart of the Center. “We are
involved with you. Be thou involved with us.”
“On three things the world stands, Torah,
Prayer and Deeds of Kindness, our house of
worship is truly a house dedicated to deeds
of kindness.”

Ritual Immersion and the
Eatinc of Tbrum a
Daring the 2nd Commonwealth,
great str«s was placed on ritual im¬
mersion, as nuv be ascertained
from halachic and apocryphal
literature. The recently discovered
ritual bath (Mihu’ii) in the Massada
excavations emphatically proves
the strict observance of these laws
of purity, even under the most
trying conditions of war and siege.
Ritual immersions were not
necessarily restricted to those in¬
stances where Biblical injunction
required them (i.c. contact with

ministration
With the advent of a new year it is time to
reappraise ourselves; to take a personal inventory of
our attitude toward our Center. Have we done what
we could or should, or have we remained complacent
and satisfied with our thoughts in the hope that others
may do their share?
We learn in the Ethics of the Fathers that Hillel
said, “Separate not thyself from the congregation/’
The interpretation given to it by our rabbis is to
identify your individual life with that of the com¬
munity and stay not apart from it. Separation is es¬
pecially unpardonable when the community or con¬
gregation is in need.
In the conflict with Amalek the hands of Moses
grew weary and they took a stone and put it under
him. “Could they not have given him a cushion or a
chair”, it is asked, but Moses said “Since my brethren
are in need, lo I will bear my part with them; for he
who bears his portion of the burden will live to see the
hour of consolation”. (Talmud)
It is fascinating to see how words written so many
years ago apply to our present day, “for he who bears
his portion of the burden”. We all have a portion of
the burden to bear to see our congregation flourish and
continue serving the needs of our congregants. In
the administration field it is my intention to see that
each member of our congregation does his share so
that the end result will be an efficient and effective
center of worship, learning and social activity. With
your cooperation we shall see the Center a spiritual
and financial success, insuring that Judaism will func¬
tion as a potent influence to our Synagogue Family
and community.
A congregation is much more than a membership
organization. It is an association of Jewish families
IRWIN WIENER
Executive Director
dedicated to high spiritual objects. It means entering
a circle of a group of men, women and youth interested
in Jewish life and creating an atmosphere of positive
action. Such is the main objective of our United
Synagogue Youth, or as I like to refer to them, our
young adults, who have undertaken the task of build¬
ing the foundation for the preservation of their identity
as Jews and the future of their synagogue. This is a
year in which we will see the youth of our congre¬
gation set the example for all of us to follow.
To the young adult and the adult I say, “The
day is short, and the work is great, and the laborers are
sluggish, but the reward is much.”
God bless us in our endeavors.
Irwin Wiener
Mrs. Mae Henderson
Comptroller

President’s Message
With the approach of a New Year, let us all
resolve to make the coming one a better year than
the last.
We have experienced a year of unpleasant events;
personal tragedies in the lives of some of our members
which affected all of us; the reduction from five to
three grades in our Day School; the resignation of our
esteemed Rabbi Arnold Turetsky, who served us de¬
votedly and faithfully for the last three years, and
under whose inspiring spiritual leadership, the Jack¬
sonville Jewish Center made great progress. Despite
all of that, we must look forward with hope, cheer and
courage, and strive to achieve a still higher standard
of Jewish consciousness through direct and personal
participation in the various phases of our Center ac¬
tivities.
One of our ‘musts’ is the elevation and intensifi¬
cation of adult education in our midst. The parents’
level of Hebrew education must at least be that of
their youngsters, otherwise, we are not building on a
solid foundation. We parents must allocate time for
Hebrew studies for the sake of our childrens’ future
in our American Jewish Community. We can do it
much more effectively by self-participation.
Since we are experiencing a severe financial crisis,
due to the higher cost of living, we ask your under¬
standing and willingness to sacrifice some of the lux¬
uries to which we have all become accustomed, and
support the Center more generously for the sake of
our traditional and spiritual survival. Let each one of
us feel a personal responsibility. This is OUR institu¬
tion. The Officers and Board of Directors are merely
your servants.
On behalf of the Congregation and myself, I want
to express our warm appreciation to Rabbi and Mrs.
Turetsky for their enrichment of Jewish content to
our Congregation, and extend our sincere good wishes
to them in all their future endeavors. May they and
their family always enjoy good health and may we
have the pleasure to hear good tidings from them.
My thanks to my predecessor, Mr. Philip Selber,
for his unlimited assistance given to me ever since I
JACK BECKER
President of the Congregation
assumed this office. The Congregation, through me,
wishes to express our deep gratitude to Mr. Ben Fried¬
man for his valuable cooperation and services; and to
our Personnel Chairman, Mr. Joseph Mizrahi and his
committee, for their diligent services which culminated
in the selection of Rabbi Jerome Lipnick as our new
spiritual leader.
Our heartiest and warmest welcome to you, Rabbi
and Mrs. Lipnick, and to your family. May your min¬
istry to our Congregation be rewarding and bring you
much spiritual fulfillment.
The Officers and Members of the Board of Direc¬
tors and the Board of Trustees join with me to extend
to each of you warm greetings and good wishes for a
year of well-being, peace and prosperity.
LESHANAH TOVAH

Rabbi Simcha Kling of Louisville, Kentucky delivered
the address at the Installation of Jack Becker as President
of the Jacksonville Jewish Center. Rabbi Kling is a personal
friend of our President and studied with him at the Seminary
during the year Mr. Becker took a Sabbatical. Rabbi Kling’s
message inspired all who attended with his “This is Your
Life” portrayal of our President.

THE CENTER CHORAL SOCIETY PICTURED AT THE ANNUAL CONCERT
Cantor Arthur Koret was the guest artist at the 18th Annual Concert
of Jewish Music, an annual event that was enjoyed by all. Proceeds realized
by his Concert were donated to the Cantors’ Institute of the Jewish Theo¬
logical Seminary of America.
Pictured below is our junior choral group, the Center Glee Club, and
is comprised of members of our Religious School performing for different
occasions during the course of the year. Both the Choral Society and Glee
Club are under the direction of Cantor Abraham Marton.
CANTOR A. MARTON

(S^enter
^Sisterhood
Torah Fund Brunch
In previous years our Center Year Book gave a
resume of what our Sisterhood accomplished during
the past year, the many affairs we sponsored and rec¬
ognition of all the women who worked. This year, not
to be different nor minimize what has been done in
the past or the many people who worked, we will look
ahead and visualize what our Sisterhood will do in
the future.
First and foremost our committee will assist our
youth, beginning with Nursery School and continuing
through Kindergarten, Foundation School, Sunday
School, Hebrew School, Girl Scouts and Brownies, Con¬
firmation Class to our ATID group (college level) and
continuing upward to our Adult Education classes,
Jewish Family Living, Israeli affairs and whatever the
needs may be to strengthen our Jewish way of life.
The above are our main concern but our members
also work towards raising funds to help support our
teaching staff and scholarships to Camp Ramah.
Patronize our Gift Shop, buy Barton’s Candy,
World Book Encyclopedias, Leagrams and have your
name embroidered on a tablecloth — this year we will
plan fund raising affairs. With our Executive Board
and the Chairman working together with all the Sister¬
hood members, we will have a rewarding and successful
year.
We also must look outside our community and
support the combined campaign for Torah Fund and
the Mathilde Schechter Residence Hall if we want our
children and future generations to get the best training
in our religion.
Changes are always a challenge, and if our pro¬
gramming and organization need revisions, we are will¬
ing to try new methods. Come to our meetings — ask
Mrs. Lewis Ansbacher, Mrs. Norman Slaff, the guest
speaker and Mrs. Jack Shorstein at the Torah Fund
Brunch.
questions — let us know your ideas — find out what
Sisterhood is and what it stands for — let us all pull
together and support our Sisterhood projects and our
children and grandchildren will benefit.
On behalf of the Officers and Board members, we
want to wish the entire congregation a Healthy, Happy
and Prosperous New Year.
Mrs. Leon Schinasi

The Center Men's Club offers its membership the
opportunity to gather in Jewish fellowship, once each
month at a dinner meeting, and participate in a pro¬
gram that coincides with the current events of the day.
Commencing with the Emmy Award winning doc¬
umentary on the “Ku Klux Klan,'' we were able to
include a program with Senators Jack Matthews and
Tom Greene and Commissioner-elect Bill Basford, as
well as that outstanding joint affair with the Sister¬
hood when they presented the thought provoking skit
entitled “Stop the Judaism, I Want to Get Off," and
a joint brotherhood meeting. The culmination of this
year's programming occurred at the final meeting
where Mayor Louis Ritter, Reverend Robert Libby and
our own Rabbi Turetsky shared their experiences of
their recent Israel Pilgrimage with the Club.
Included in the price of Men's Club membership
is the annual paid-up affair Dinner-Dance, which in¬
cludes a free cocktail party as part of the membership
fee.
The Center Men's Club assists weekly in the pre¬
paring and serving of breakfast to the Minyan Club
and the years' activities are climaxed with the annual
R. ROBERT SHORSTEIN,
President
fund raising Sunday School Picnic held in conjunction
with the opening of the Center Day Campsite. The
net proceeds from the Picnic are used to support youth
activities at the Center, with emphasis on Camp
Ramah.
Special thanks are due this year to the Food
Chairman David Safer and his committee, to S. Robert
Shorstein, Stanley Kantor and the entire Men's Club
membership for their active participation.

Mr. Burton I. Grossman, Chairman of the Board, presenting a gift to
Bob and Linda Fischer on behalf of the congregation for seven wonderful
years of dedication and devotion to his job as Youth Director and Execu¬
tive Director of the Jacksonville Jewish Center.
Seven years of loyal devotion — For seven years the
Jacksonville Jewish Center was fortunate to have in
its midst a man with drive and dedication to his job,
his Center and his people. One cannot think of the
Jacksonville Jewish Center and not remember Bob
and Linda and all that they accomplished in their ef¬
forts for a more meaningful program for their congre¬
gants. To name just a few of the highlights of his
accomplishments one need only drive out State Road
13 and as you approach a sign indicating Center Day
Camp marvel at its success and beauty; one need
only visit with the youth of the congregation and see
the inspiration they received from Bob Fischer por¬
trayed in their actions and deeds; one need only visit
our Gym in the evenings or on weekends and realize
that the athletic program is a success because of his
endeavors. We hope that Bob will remember us as we
remember him.
Mr. Jack Becker, newly installed President, welcomes Mr. Irwin Wiener,
the new Executive Director and bids farewell to Bob Fischer and wishes
him success in his new venture.

===== PAGE 48 =====
Rabbi Arnold S. Turetsky was the Rabbi of the
Jacksonville Jewish Center for the past three years.
The years have flown by swiftly but the accomplish¬
ments achieved will remain with us for a long time
to come.
His three years of service at the Jacksonville
Jewish Center were marked with increased attendance
at Shabbat Services, a revitalized adult education
program, the creation and development of our ATID
group which started as an experiment in June of 1965
and to date is operating with a great deal of effective¬
ness and success, the personal visits to the campus
grounds in Gainesville which made a bridge between
the students at school and their home synagogue. The
Rabbi and his wife, Noemie, will long be remembered
for the devoted way in which they instilled Judaism
among our members.
We truly will miss Rabbi Turetsky and his family,
and for these three years we say “Todah Rabah".
Rabbi Arnold S. Turetsky
iScope oj ¿inactivities
Religious Services — Traditional prayer services are
held daily, morning and evening in the Chapel. Friday
Family Services, Sabbath and Holiday Services are
observed in the Sanctuary throughout the year.
Personal Religious Observances — Traditional Jewish
customs are observed in connection with Circumcision,
naming of girls,, Pidyon Ha-ben, Bar Mitzvah, Bat
Mitzvah, Confirmation, Marriages, ,Conversion of Jud¬
aism, Bereavement.
Education — Daily Kindergarten and Nursery with
outstanding program of secular and religious content.
Hebrew School, carefully graded with intensive He¬
brew content. Sunday School, with a curriculum includ¬
ing Jewish history and customs, Solomon Schechter
Day School, a graded secular and Hebrew School, 1st
through 5th grade, with Pre-Jr., Junior, Teen and
Confirmation Congregations and Minyan Clubs.
Extension Services — Library, outstanding collection
of juvenile and adult Jewish books; Adult Education,
religious and cultural Jewish courses; Music, programs
of religious and cultural Jewish music; Sisterhood and
Men's Club; Social activities provided by Mr. and Mrs.
Club, Amity Club, “32 Club", Regency Club; Family
Recreation Program (Olympic Pool), summer week¬
days and Sundays for all ages at our Campsite.
Youth Activities — ATID for college age youth, United
Synagogue Youth, various age levels are served by
Center Youth League, Charles Palot Club, Major's Pre-
Teens, Tween-Agers; Scouting (Boy Scouts, Cub
Scouts, Brownies and Girl Scouts); Athletics, pro¬
grams for youth and adults; Day Camp — nine weeks
summer program at our 36 acre Campsite.

RELIGIOUS EDUCATION
JACK SHORSTEIN
Chairman
TED GROSS
Principal
LOUIS SAFER
Co-Chairman
This is an age when every basic value is put to
question by teen-agers. Great stress has therefore been
placed in our curricular development on the High
School years. There were two obstacles: irregular begin¬
nings in the elementary department, and irregular at¬
tendance in the advanced department. A Code of
Requirements was therefore published and distributed
to all members. Comments on this Code from national
and regional sources have described it as courageous,
resourceful, and a milestone in the development of
Jewish education in Amrica.
Our Hebrew High School students have enabled
us to place Haftorah instruction where it belongs: as
training for the Jr. Congregational system. Thus Bar
and Bas Mitzvah candidates are expected to prepare
themselves for their ceremonies, with only occasional
aid from members of the staff.
In the Sunday Religious School, the entire High
School Department was concentrated in the hands
of a limited number of capable teachers, who have
much to give to their students. Together with con¬
trolled discipline procedures this has resulted in the
most effective teaching program ever offered on Sun¬
MRS.
SHELDON GENDZIER
Administrative Assistant
day mornings.
The Ramah summer camping scholarship program
of the Center has been published by the Southeastern
regional office of the United Synagogue and is being
used as a guide by many congregations. There are 18
prospective candidates for the 1967 summer season.
With 22 of our pupils in the Post Bar Mitzvah
Torah Reading Group, it has become common practice
to have them take over this function throughout the
year, except on Sabbaths when a pupil is being Bar
Mitzvah.
The Daily Kindergarten reached the highest en¬
rollment in its history attaining the number of 105
pupils and has resulted in three separate classes for
the five-year-old group.
In his visit here, the Assistant Director of the
United Synagogue Commission on Jewish Education
expressed surprise at the unusually extended activities
accepted by the members of our staff. We are thank¬
ful to them and to the members of the Educational
Committee under the chairmanship of Mr. Jack Shor-
stein for their devotd efforts.
MRS. A. ROSENZWEIG
School Secretary

RELIGIOUS EDUCATION
First graders with their teacher, Mrs. Nathan Sandler
A complete education within one general
framework to meet the needs of Jewish
students makes the Foundation School the
shining jewel in our educational system. No
desirable educational endeavor has been
neglected. Library trips and field trips have
been arranged. On Jewish holidays the Foun¬
dation family has planned many home-like
functions.
The Foundation School this year con¬
ducted a successful experiment in conducting
a 4th and 5th grade together. Much com¬
mendation has been bestowed on the pioneer¬
ing efforts of the teachers, and the parents
who worked wholeheartedly with them.
Mrs. Norman Moss and her students
Accomplishments were also learning ac¬
tivities in the Hebrew and General studies
department.
Reports of our progress at the First
National Conference of North American Day
Schools in December indicated that not only
is our Day School one of the least expensive,
but our pupils receive more extended induc¬
tion, such as the time for English studies. By
meeting the standards of the national office,
our school was awarded a certificate of attain¬
ment and the use of the name Solomon
Schechter Day School.
4th and 5th garders shown in the classroom with
Mrs. Sheldon Gendzier and Mrs. Herbert Plotkin.
Mrs. Leo Moskovitz and her class of pupils.

Mr. Alan Gordon, on behalf of his mother, Mrs.
Robert J. Gordon, and his brother, Samuel Gordon,
presents the Robert Gordon Leadership Grant for 1966
to Barbara Talpalar and Jerald Morris, as Rabbi Turet¬
sky observes. This scholarship, begun in 1965, in mem¬
ory of one of the most distinguished lay leaders of the
center, is awarded to the student or students with the
most promising leadership potential, and provides for
attendance at Camp Ramah. These two students were
among the eighteen youngsters who left Jacksonville
for a summer at Camp Ramah, in Wingdale, N. Y.,
and Palmer, Mass.
The students who went to Camp Ramah were:
Earl Berman, Martin Berman, Mayer Becker, Belinda
Felhandler, Jeff Goldstein, Carol Gross, Ricki Gross,
Stanley Halpern, Jeffrey Morris, Jerald Morris, Sharon
Raskin, Margaret Rose, Cynthia Safer, Paula Saraga,
Harry Sinoff, Barbara Talpalar, Barry and Jonathan
Turetsky.

zz/^fctivi
v it íes
MRS. LOUIS SLOTT
Center Youth Chairman
IRWIN WIENER
Youth Director
GEORGE GLOSSMAN
Asst. Youth Director
At the Center a great deal of emphasis is placed
on the proper training and direction of the youth in
the congregation.
To insure its own future, every organization and
every movement needs to sponsor a youth department.
But, as stated in an editorial in the United Synagogue
Review, winter 1963 . . . “There is something distinc¬
tive about our concept of youth work which is unique
to the Conservative Movement. We have never re¬
garded youth work as merely group work or recrea¬
tional work. In our scheme of things, our all pervading
purpose is to bring the message of Judaism to our
young people, and everything in which we are engaged
is aimed at this purpose. In a real sense, therefore, our
youth work is educational work — informal education,
to be sure, but educational work nevertheless.” As
interested members of our congregation, we should not
lose sight of the fact that U. S. Y. should be treated
as an integral part of the educational program at the
Center.
The puropse of a U. S. Y. program is to offer to
our post-Bar Mitzvah youth social, cultural, educa¬
tional and religious activities on an informal, more
“palatable” level.
Obviously, U. S. Y. is not the only means of keep¬
ing children close to a synagogue and increasing a
child’s identification with Judaism. But it is the most
comprehensive method presently available. The sec-
cess of any particular U. S. Y. chapter is dependent
on the attitude taken by both the youth and their
parents. It is certainly dependent in large measure on
the active support of the Congregation — financially,
physically and personally.
This past year a National Pre USY Chapter was
chartered at the Center. It was formed from the com¬
bination of Major’s Pre-Teens and TweenAgers.
We look forward to another successful year of
accomplishments.
GEORGE GLOSSMAN
Assistant Youth Director
*

rejan
izations
Center Youth League (CYL) — A co-ed USY Chapter
for 11th and 12th graders meeting twice monthly on
Sunday from September to May.
Charles Palot Club (CPC) — USY Chapter for 9th
and 10th grade boys and girls, meeting throughout the
school year twice monthly on Sundays at the Center.
Major’s Pre-USY — A co-ed youth club for 5th to 8th
graders with varied social, religious, and athletic func¬
tions held monthly.
To this list is also added our sponsorship of Boy
Scouts ,Troop 14), Cub Scouts (Pack 14), Girl Scouts
(Troops 6 and 193) and Brownie Troops 219 and 269).

CENTER YOUTH LEAGUE
Officers of C. Y. L.: Miss Rita Weinstein, Advisor; Debby Morris, Morrey Edwards,
Gilda Goldman, Eliot Safer (President), Hilda Goldman, Gary Wolfson and Sherri Silverman.
Under the excellent leadership of Leonard Selber,
the Center Youth League began the year’s activities in
July. Throughout the summer informal groups met and
discussed current problems facing our Jewish youth.
Our U. S. Y.’ers went off to L. T. I. with renewed
spirits and returned to hold a gala opening dance and
beach party.
This year’s Tri-City Convention was hosted by
Jacksonville with Leon Yergin, Tri-City President,
Gail Price and Mendy Marks, the Convention Co-
Chairmen working towards a successful Conclave.
Throughout the year discussion groups continued,
along with several fund raising projects. In March the
annual Passover macaroon sale paved the way for the
establishment of a U. S. Y. Scholarship Fund. Jack
Mizrahi helped double last year’s sales which will pro¬
vide scholarships for deserving members to attend
conclaves. The first scholarships were given to Leonard
Selber, Jack Mizrahi and Peggy Cohen for the Re¬
gional Convention in Atlanta.
The year came to a close with the Annual Ban¬
quet and Installation of next year’s officers. It marked
the departure of many faithful U. S. Y. workers and
the arrival of a new group of outstanding leaders.
Jack Mizrahi and Gail Price were the recipients of
the awards for outstanding boy and girl in the Center
Youth League.
We are looking forward to the success of the
coming year under new leadership and the added im¬
petus of having three newly chosen Regional Chairman
in our midst.
CHARLES PALOT CLUB
Officers of C. P. C.: Wendy Fraden, Barbara Talpalar, Mayer Becker (President),
Donna Price, Cynthia Safer, Jeffery Morris, and Mrs. Rene Slott Montaigne.
There are three main facets of United Synagogue
Youth: Learning, helping others and having a good
time with our Jewish friends. Through our programs,
Charles Palot Club was active in all three respects.
We had many programs on study this year, both
religious and secular. Our study groups after Friday
evening services entitled us to a Regional study group
award. We had a speaker from Israeli bonds, a repre¬
sentative from the Florida Highway Patrol and a
Psychologist speak to us.
One of the most worthwhile activities was con¬
ducting services for River Garden one Shabbos, and
then entertaining the residents with Israeli singing and
dancing. We sponsored a booth at the Sisterhood Han-
ukah Fair and had a car wash to raise money for
Building Spiritual Bridges. This year at Regional Con¬
vention in Atlanta our sixteen delegates presented the
region with a check for $150 for B. S. B. We had
dances, a beach party and bowling-dinner parties.
As you can see, this past year has been a most
busy and successful one for C. P. C. Many thanks are
due our Advisor, Mrs. Rene Slott Montaigne, for all
her efforts on behalf of our organization.
This summer also will be active for U. S. Y.’ers.
Besides our eighteen delegates to L. T. I. at Hender¬
sonville, N. C., one other U. S. Y’er, Eliot Safer, is
going on U. S. Y. on Wheels for six weeks, touring
the United States with about 40 other USY’ers.

roop
Boy Scout Troop pictured with Scoutmaster Henry Lasris, Leon Zimmerman and Ted Freedman, Institutional
Representative.
HARRY KOWITZ,
Webelos Leader
Rabbi A. S. Turetsky, Henry Lasris and Dr.
Harry Yergin with Ner Tamid award winners
Mark Zimmerman and Bruce Blumberg.
HENRY LASRIS,
Scoutmaster
Troop 14 has just completed one of its most active years
under the direction of Henry Lasris, Scoutmaster, and his
dedicated committee of fathers.
The year opened with a trip to visit the local professional
football team. This was followed by a cook-out and swim party
at the Center campsite. The Chairman for this affair was
Irwin Smith. A month later the Troop participated in the
annual Camporee at Silver Springs. Among the highlights of
this Camporee was a visit to the Snake Farm and competition
in the various Scouting events.
Two Scouts received the Ner Tamid award. The recipients
Bruce Blumberg and Mark Zimmerman were ably taught by
Dr. Harry Yergin. Through the courtesy of the Center and
the Men’s Club the Troop purchased sixteen two-man tents.
The tents were initiated on a field trip to Gold Head State
Park.
The Boy Scouts contributed to the Center Sisterhood
Bazaar with a booth and they also contributed to the Men’s
Club Picnic by building a monkey bridge which was one of the
highlights. The year was climaxed with sixteen boys attending
a week at Boy Scout Camp at Orange Park, achieving advance¬
ment in rank and merit. Earl Berman and Charles Trager
were elected to the Order of the Arrow.
There were many other field trips during the year to such
places as Crandall Point, Fort Clinch and Black Creek. Jeffrey
Goldstein was awarded the Eagle Scout rank.
TROOP 14 SCOUT LEADERS
Scoutmaster Henry Lasris
Asst. Scoutmasters Harry Berman and Leon Zimmerman
Chairman of Troop Committee Irwin Smith
Chairman of Board of Review Dr. Harry Yergin
Committeemen George Glossman and Gus Gross

With enthusiasm and devotion by all concerned,
the Pack has remained among the leaders in the city.

Director Robert Fischer, our past Cubmaster Sidney

and overworked Chief Den Mother. Best Cub awards

Michael Nykerk.

Girl Scouts with Mrs. Herbert Lewitt, Leader
The Center Sisterhood Girl Scout Program, spon¬
sored by the Sisterhood under the chairmanship of
Mrs. Jack Price, completed its season in May. The
Arlington Junior Girl Scouts and the Southside Brown¬
ies consisted of 29 girls.
The Juniors, Troop No. 210, led by Mrs. Morton
Benjamin, Mrs. Arthur Eisen and Mrs. Marvin Hal-
pern had several outings to places such as Camp Chow-
enwaw, Boone Park and Fort Caroline; made yarn dolls
for the children at Hope Haven Hospital; received an
honor award for cookie sales; made pencil holders, leaf
prints and learned the art of outdoor camping.
The Brownies, Troop No. 269, led by Mrs. Herbert
Lewitt, celebrated Tu B’shevat by planting a tree at
River Garden Home; visited Cohen’s Candy Kitchen;
made menorahs for their homes; planted and harvested
a string bean garden and enjoyed an active Scouting
year.
Sincere thanks are due our leaders who enthus¬
iastically led our girls through an active and fruitful
season.

enter
2\v/ C
amp
Located on a beautifully wooded 36 acre tract of
land on State Road No. 13, the Center Day Camp has
completed a very successful 21st season.
Under the supervision of Mrs. Louis Slott, Direc¬
tor, and Jerrold Cohn, Assistant Director, a well diver¬
sified program of the finest instruction in Water sports,
Arts and Crafts, Nature, Athletics and physical fitness
has been offered to all Jewish children between the
ages of 4 to 14.
Our qualified staff who have been trained in our
own Counselor-in-Training program have done an ex¬
cellent job in giving special attention to the needs of
the individual camper.
Directed by Bruce Yergin, our Red Cross trained
instructors have taught many to swim and all campers
to feel at home in the water. Asa member of the Na¬
tional Water Skiing Association, our Camp, under the
capable direction of Barry Setzer, has offered a full
program of skiing to our campers.
Arts and Crafts, supervised by Mrs. M. J. Reiser
(Aunt Tillie) and her well trained staff, offer the finest
program of any Camp in this area.
Mrs. Richard Nykerk, Secretary; Jerrold Cohn, Assistant
Director; Mrs. Louis Slott, Director
As a progressive Camp we are constantly adding
to our program and this year the special Shabbat
observance on Friday, with the entire Camp participat¬
ing in prayer, games and Israeli songs and dances,, has
been highly accepted.
Other popular additions to our program have been
the monkey rope, out-of-door sketching in pencil and
charcoal and taking care of the camp pets, rabbits,
chickens and hamsters.
Listed among the most popular trips arranged by
the program director, Jack Fleet, have been the Chil¬
dren’s Museum, Zoo, Cummer Art Gallery, Haydon
Bums Library, Coca-Cola, Frito-Lays„ Bowling and
the Kiddie Show at Florida Theatre.
As our Comp continues to grow we look forward COUNSELOR-IN-TRAINING Class with their Instructor Jerrold
to becoming the largest and finest Day Camp in the Cohn. Best C. I. T. Awards presented to Arlene Dittman and
southeast. Michael Witten.

ATID has just completed its second successful year under the leadership and guidance of Rabbi

1966-1967 OFFICERS AND BOARD
President Mr. and Mrs. Nathan Goldstein

With its dual goal of spreading friendship and en¬
abling married couples to share good times, the Mr.
and Mrs. Club is now in its 13th successful year.
Each month, exciting and novel affairs are held and
guided through the leadership of committees formed
to arrange each gala event.
The themes vary from month to month, but tradi¬
tional events include an extravagant New Year’s Eve
party, a mid-year Installation Banquet, and a family
picnic or outing during the summer months.
As in all other social clubs, there is a central core
of officers and board members, and the organization
is governed by husbands and wives serving and work¬
ing together. Through each member’s sharing the re¬
sponsibility of serving on a monthly committee, an
atmosphere of warm congeniality always prevails, and
the promotion of good fellowship is the end result.
All couples who are interested in joining and
sharing all the wonderful things offered by the Mr.
and Mrs. Club are urged to contact any of the club's
officers or board members.

The Thirty-Two Club, an organization to provide
fellowship between young couples of the Jewish faith
through social activities, has just celebrated its fifth
birthday. The group, which meets on the first Satur¬
day evening of each month, has varied parties ranging
from the “Georgia-Florida Football Game Party” to
the “Gala New Years Eve Bair’. Interested couples
may contact this year’s president, Stanley Davidow,
concerning membership.

The Tofield Memorial Library Committee is always
striving to involve many members of our congregation.
One of the new programs initiated this year was pub¬
lishing book reviews of recently acquired current books
in the Center Bulletin when space permitted. These
short sketches by Howard Haber, Publicity Chairman
for the library, have made the membership more aware
of the library’s many facilities and the outstanding
books which are offersd.
Appropriate books were made available again for the
summer day camp as was done in 1965. The campers
Members of the Library Committee
and the counsellors made use of these books during the
camp session and especially during inclement weather.
The additional library hours on Friday evenings fol¬
lowing services, Wednesday evenings in conjunction
with Adult Education classes and during day time
school hours have made our books more readily avail¬
able to a greater number of our congregants.
The library is open daily except Saturday. Many
thanks are due the committee members who work
throughout the year to make our library among the
best equipped Jewish libraries.

Our maintenance staff shoulders one of the heav¬
iest burdens in our Center program, that of maintain¬
ing our facilities so that they are always in a present¬
able state for our use. Pictured at the right are Tim
Riley and Annie Mae Fort who have been loyal and
devoted members of our maintenance crew for a com-
bind total of 38 years.
Tim Riley coordinates all maintenance at the Cen¬
ter under the direction of our Executive Director.
Annie Mae Fort maintains our kitchen and prepares
our meals, not only for our adults but also for our vast
food program for our school children.
Also preparing tasty food at many of the Center’s
affairs and members’ simchas are three of our local
caterers: Mr. and Mrs. Albert Meyerhoff, Mrs. Mary
Safer and Mrs. Lena Berkowitz.
Our appreciation to this loyal and devoted staff
who have given many years of unselfish service to our
Synagogue-Center.

Shown above is our transportation staff consisting
of Morris Montford, a veteran of 20 years, and two
new members of our transportation staff, Henry Hill
and Frank Powell, who team together to transport our
students from their schools and homes to classes at
the Center.
The transprtation system of routes, and mainten¬
ance, is under the supervision of our Transportation
Committee with Herbert Lewitt, Chairman.

Pr esident
Serving the entire Jewish community of Jackson¬
ville, the Jewish Community Council is the central
Welfare Fund and Community Relations body of the
Jewish community. An association of individuals,
synagogues, organizations and social service agencies,
the program encompasses key activities which give
unity, cohesion and coordination to local Jewish com¬
munal life.
A community-wide annual Welfare Fund Cam¬
paign provides support to significant local, national
and overseas organizations. The National United Jew¬
ish Appeal has expressed American Jewry’s respon¬
sibility for the welfare and rehabilitation needs of
Jewish communities the world over for over 20 years.
River Garden Hebrew Home for the Aged and the
Jewish Welfare Society are the local beneficiaries.
The Council publishes a monthly newspaper known
as the Commentator. It is the official organ of the
Council and its member organizations.
The Council conducts a leisure time activities
program which includes Youth Council programs such
as college preparatory workshops, a youth news page
in the Commntator, and a youth division in the an¬
nual Jewish Welfare Fund Campaign. The Council
also provides a community summer Day Camp for
children between the ages of 4 and 15.

RIVER GARDEN HOME
SAMUEL J. THIER, President
O F F I (

❖ ❖ ❖
1946 - 1966
Two decades of River Garden’s service to the aged and
infirm was celebrated and honored in 1966. It was an occasion
of rejoicing that so much was accomplished and an occasion of
reflecting on what is ahead in the field of aging.
In 1946, River Garden was a brand new social experiment
in community responsibility for the care of some of its elderly
members. It was not so new since the fifth Mosaic command¬
ment, “Honor thy mother and father” had been in vogue for
about 5,000 years; but the idea of a home for the aged for small
Florida communities was not the most popular of projects.
Social needs quickly proved the validity of the dreams
of that gallant group of women in Ladies Hebrew Sheltering
Aid Society. Their efforts were first justified in 1947 when
ground was broken for the South Building, and again when
ground was broken in 1955 for the North Building.
E R S
Mrs. David Meyerhardt Vice President
Chairman, Residents Welfare
Dr. Harry Yoffee Vice President, Medical Director
Norman Hofheimer Secretary
Sol Katz Treasurer
Sidney Entman Executive Director
Lily Volotin Administrative Assistant
HONORARY PRESIDENTS
Ira Stein Irving Klepper
Harry Gendzier Joe Becker
Dr. Sam Witten Arthur J. Gutman
* *
The final approval and accolades for their vision occurred
when the original wooden mansion was replaced by the 4-story
Wolfson Memorial Building. Thus, from a modest faciilty
housing eleven elderly people, the Home now accommodates
110 aged and infirm residents in up-to-date and modern
buildings.
Buildings in themselves do not provide living experiences
but are only vehicles in which activities take place. It is the
broad gamut of medical, nursing, religious, recreational, social
and personal services which create the warmth and efficiency
of a “home”. It is staff, volunteers, and the community who
care which create the climate of River Garden.
As the Home faces the challenges of the next decade, we
do know that we will soon build again as plans for the Eugenia
Coleman Memorial Building are being formulated. The idea
of housing for the elderly is still maturing. Other projects
and other needs as they become formalized will be met with
the same zeal and ardor as demonstrated these past twenty
years.

1966-1967
OFFICERS
President Mrs. Roy J. Sloat
First Vice-President Mrs. I. P. Edwards
Second Vice-President Mrs. Myron Rosenberg
Third Vice-President Mrs. J. Paul Witten
Recording Secretary Mrs. Larry Richter
Financial Serretary Mrs. Albert Kofka
Corresponding Secretary Mrs. Sam Lazarus
Treasurer (General) Mrs. Sam Siegel
Treasurer (Projects) Mrs. Louis Witten
River Garden Auxiliary is entering its 43rd year
of “Service with a Heart”. Formerly known as Ladies
Hebrew Sheltering Aid Society, this organization was
founded by a group of dedicated women who dreamed
of having a home for Jewish aged in Jacksonville. In
1946 their dream became a reality and last year by
unanimous vote, the name of Ladies Aid was offi¬
cially changed to River Garden Auxiliary befitting the
primary role of the organization.
The Auxiliary provides the extra budgetary needs
as furnishing, redecorating, air conditioning, kitchen
and infirmary equipment, and hobby shop materials.
Funds are raised through our Tombola, Donor, social
printing projects, as well as annual dues and life mem¬
berships. Linen at the Home is replenished through
our annual Linen Shower affair.
In the field of service, Ladies Aid sponsors activity
and volunteer programs. Residents participate in proj¬
ects served by the Auxiliary such as ceramic and art
classes, hobby shop, resident’s magazine. Special par¬
ties are planned for Mother’s Day, Father’s Day and
Chanukah. Personal service to the residents is rendered
Officers of River Garden Auxiliary
by our Red Cross volunteers. Our members also pro¬
vide door-to-door transportation for non residents to
attend the weekly Golden Sunshine meetings, held at
River Garden. This autonomous group emerged from
River Garden Auxiliary sponsored weekly senior citi¬
zens meetings. We continue to lend support and guid¬
ance to the group. A monthly bridge party is held
providing residents the opportunity to play bridge with
members of the Auxiliary.
As our new year rolls around, we pledge our con¬
tinued devotion and service to River Garden and its
residents in the hope that we can help bring some
measure of comfort and meaning to their lives.

Executive Director .... Lawrence Rackow
LAWRENCE RACKOW
— FOR ALL PEOPLE —
1. Family and Individual Counseling
2. Adoption Program
3. Foster Home Care
4. Services to Senior Citizens
5. Employment and Vocational Counseling
6. New American Resettlement and Restitution Pro¬
grams
7. Short Term Financial Assistance
8. Transient Program (for persons passing through
town)
9. Program to help Persons in Prison
10. Day Camp and College Scholarship Programs
11. Friendly Visiting Program
Interpersonal problems cut through all levels. Rich
or poor, highly educated or illiterate, prominent or
humble, there are times and situations when help is
is needed. A professional staff, alone or backed by the
skills and services of the many cooperating doctors,
dentists, lawyers and rabbis and the varied services of
its sister agencies locally and nationally, are available
to help the individuals find solutions to many vexing
probems.
Fonded in 1917 as the United Jewish Charities,
the Jewish Family & Children’s Services, Inc., is now
entering its 50th year of service. For the youth pro¬
fession of social work and particularly for the small
community in which it grew, its history is compara¬
tively long and rich — reflecting both the traditional
Jewish emphasis on family life and tsedakah, and the
constant search to absorb and utilize the current in¬
sights to meet the ever changing needs of individuals
which make up the community.
Our present emphasis on services as a right, for
all levels of the economic and social ladder is a far
cry from “charity for the needy and the worthy poor”
as are the varied confidential professional services of
the staff from the limited activities of half a century
ago. The names, need, times and techniques have
changed but the deeply ingrained concern for one’s
fellow man and the modest but pioneering spirit that
caused the Jewish Welfare Society to be the first
and, for many years, the only Jewish licensed child
placing agent in the state, that caused it to explore
the needs for a child guidance clinic and to start a
demonstrating non-sectarian agency in the only city
in the United States that did not have one, are inspir¬
ations worthy of emulation by each generation.

SIDNEY WEISS, President
The essential function of the Zionist Organization
of America is to keep alive in this country an interest
in the State of Israel. This interest is many-sided, as
the efforts of Zionism always were. The political ob¬
jective was the creation of a Jewish State. To this end
it was necessary to revive the Hebrew language, ac¬
quire land, train colonists for settlement and raise
funds to finance the project. All this the Zionist move¬
ment did.
Today, the Z. 0. A. continues to foster support for
the State of Israel. Moreover, the Z. 0. A. seeks to
strengthen the bonds between American Jewry and
the Jewish State on the basis of the cultural tradition
that is our common heritage.
Religious differences present no obstacle as Zion¬
ism encompasses all shades of religious belief in every
global area.
The Z. 0. A. invites all Jewish men to join its
ranks.

The local chapter of Hadassah was formed in
1925. It is one of the largest organizations for Jewish
women in Jacksonville. Almost 750 women boast
membership in the chapter and annually seek to raise
quotas totaling nearly $17,500.
The chief fund raising project for this year will
be a Thrift Shop. Merchandise in top notch condition
will be sold. Another new innovation is the forming of
a young matrons group.
Hadassah projects are two-fold. On the American
scene, an educational program, a youth activities pro¬
gram, the Young Judaeans, Junior Hadassah, co-
Mrs. Abe Newman, President
sponsored with Z. O. A., and Camp Judaea in Hender¬
sonville, North Carolina. Hadassah Medical Organiza¬
tion, Youth Alipah, Jewish National Fund, and Voca¬
tional Education are its projects in Israel.
Hadassah meets on the second Tuesday of each
month from September through May. This year's cal¬
endar will offer a variety of activities to meet the
educational and social needs of each member.
The Jacksonville Chapter announces a new group
for young women, under the chairmanship of Mrs.
Lewis Young.

This evening group of Hadassah comprises over
100 members, most of whom are business and profes¬
sional women who are only able to attend evening
meetings, but also many non-working women for
whom evening meetings are more convenient. Meet¬
ings are in the Jacksonville Jewish Center the second
Monday of each month. Fund raising and education
are stressed with monthly programs highlighting these
themes. A very successful season was terminated with
a dinner meeting at the Jacksonville Jewish Center,
catered by Mrs. Philip Gottlieb.

MRS. JOEL GOLDMAN
The members of the Jacksonville Section of the
National Council of Jewish Women ask the community
for their assistance in carrying out the various projects:
1. Juvenile Shelter — We are the only civic group
offering planned, year-round programs and activities for
children desperately in need of help.
2. We collect and donate new clothing for indig¬
ent children.
3. Mental Health Fair — Our annual program and
and seminar, jointly sponsored with the Mental Health
Association of Duval County, increases public under¬
standing in this field.
4. Teachers' Aid — Our trained members aid
classroom teachers with academic instruction of chil¬
dren with speech and hearing handicaps.
5. Women in Community Service (WICS) — We
screen underprivileged girls ages 16 thru 21 for the
Job Corps Training Centers.
6. Sewing Committee — We make layettes for
distribution to the needy.
7. Medical Loan Shelf — Through the Visiting
Nurse Association, we supply hospital and sick room
equipment to the indigent in our community.
8. Senior Citizens Program — We sponsor “Edu¬
cation Day" for Senior Citizens. Leading doctors and
social workers conduct this free program for all Senior
Citizens groups.
9. Pre-school Vision Screening — Our trained vol¬
unteers test the eyes of pre-school children in Duval
County for Ambalyopia (lazy eye blindness).

Bert Whitfield, President
“The cycle of Brotherly Love, Benevolence and Harmony will be never ending”
No greater reward can be received than the joy and
satisfaction resulting from service to our brothers and
sisters. By such effort our own B’nai B’rith and the
entire community will benefit.
B’nai B’rith is a world-wide organization of many
facets, respected and revered by all, and joined with
dignity by Jews everywhere.
The following are some of the far-flung activities
of B’nai B’rith:
1. Contribution to citizenship by training youth, co¬
operation with other groups.
2. Hillel Foundation — the means of moving the young
people on campus back to Jewish life.
3. Vocational service — assisting Jews toward econ¬
omic development.
4. Patriotism — B’nai B’rith went into action from
the Civil War through each of the conflicts. Volun¬
teer workers served in bringing hope and rehabilita¬
tion to the armed forces and veterans.
5. B’nai B’rith Youth Organization — Founded in 1924
because of a concern over the vast number of young
Jews who were drifting away from Judaism. The
leaders of tomorrow are trained in this organization.
6. The Anti-Defamation League, Defender of Rights,
was founded in 1913 by a brilliant lawyer. His
theory of understanding through enlightenment is
the foundation for the League’s approach to cur¬
rent problems. Must men hate?

B’nai B’rith Women is synonomous with its motto
“Service, Fidelity, Devotion.” There are many philan-
trophies which rely on B’nai B’rith for their support.
In America they are National Jewish Hospital in
Denver, Colorado, Bellefaire Children’s Home for emo¬
tionally disturbed youngsters, in Cleveland, Ohio—in
Hot Springs, Arkansas, the Leo N. Levi Hospital, B’nai
B’rith Family Center at Mayo Clinic and the Salk
Foundation.
In Israel, B’nai B’rith sponsors two Kibbutzim,
Childrens Home for emotionally disturbed youngsters,
Martyr’s Forest and Hillel Foundation.
In Duval County we have seen a tremendous re¬
sponse for just one of the aspects of B’nai B’rith. A
Hillel Counselorship is in the process of establishment
at Jacksonville University through the combined ef¬
forts of B’nai B’rith Women, the B’nai B’rith Lodges
and the Jacksonville Jewish Community Council. This
will offer to the Jewish students at Jacksonville Uni¬
versity “A Home Away From Home.” At Duval Medi¬
cal Center we have given our services in seeing to it
that the Mental Theraphy Dept, and the Pharmaceu¬
tical Dept, were supplied with pill bottles, baby food
jars, and torn clean nylon hose to be used by the
Center. Through our efforts the Florida State Associa¬
tion of B’nai B’rith Women have aided us in this
project and have undertaken the collection of same
all directed towards Jacksonville’s Duval Medical Cen¬
ter.
B’nai B’rith Women work at the River Garden
Hebrew Home for the Aged, on the United Jewish
Appeal, Israel Bond Drive, Lake City Veterans Hos¬
pital, and any and all assorted drives such as the
United Nations Day Luncheon under Citizenship, Vet¬
erans Service.
This year we are continuing to distribute 1,600
Calendar Memo Date Books to the public schools
through the AntiDefamation League in conjunction
with the Jewish Community Council.
Another successful program constantly perpetu¬
ated is the “Dolls for Democracy program.”

HENRY ROGOZINSKI,
President
This past year “Gators” A.Z.A. No. 272 was proud
to send its largest delegation in 30 years. There the
charter was presented by Eliot Borkson and Alan
Harris, who won first place in the debating contest.
Throughout the year our Chapter has tried to
fulfill its obligation to the national program. “Gators”
AZA has done this by working with such programs as
the road block for the M.D.A., hosting program with
speakers like Judge Lamar Weingeart and holding
unique social affairs.
A.Z.A. is a fraternal order of young Jewish men
who are dedicated to preserving our seven cardinal
principals: Conduct, Patriotism, Judaism, Fraternity,
Filial Love and Charity.
“Gators” A.Z.A. has concluded a well rounded and
beneficial year and expects to go further in future
years. “Gators” would like to thank Mr. Myron Blatt-
ner who was our past Advisor, for all that he did for
our Chapter. Also we would like to express our grati¬
tude to our sister Chapter “Gators” B.B.G , for making
this year what it was.

LINDA HILSENRAD,
President
During the summer months Gator B’nai B’rith
Girls were very active. First they participated in Proj¬
ect Headstart and then had the annual invitational
tea for prospective members. In November the girls
went out Halloween night and collected candy for the
children in Brewster Hospital, Hope Haven and Duval
Medical Center. Along with our brother A.Z.A.’s we
collected over $400 for the Muscular Distrophy drive.
At regional Convention our newspaper took second
place in competition; our alma mater placed third; and
Sharon Raskin won first place in the oratory contest.
By means of a raffle Gators collected $115 which they
donated to the International Service Fund. We con¬
cluded our official year with an Installation Luncheon
for the newly elected officers at the Holiday Inn.
Superlatives were presented and new officers installed.

The Progressive Credit Union sponsored by the
Jewish Progressive Society is organized to encourage
thrift and provide a safe, convenient medium where¬
by members can invest and accumulate savings. The
Credit Union carries life savings insurance of $2,000
and loan protection insurance without any direct cost
to the members. Last year the Board declared a 3%
dividend on savings and 10% rebate of interest to
borrowing members.
Ten Commandments of our Credit Union: The
Member is the most important person in our Credit
Union. The Member is not an outsider, he is an owner
of our Credit Union. The Member is not an interrup¬
tion of our work, he is the purpose of it. The Member
does us a favor when he calls — We are not doing
(Credit XÁ
nion
The Progressive Credit Union Officers
him a favor whn we serve him. The Member is not
a cold statistic — He is a human being with feelings
and emotions like our own. The Member is not some¬
one to argue or match wits with. The Member is a
person who brings us his wants — it is our job to
fill these wants. The Member is deserving of the most
courteous and attentive treatment we can give him.
The Member is not dependent on us, we are dependent
on him. The Member is the life blood of this Credit
Union.

meeting at the Center. Anyone who wishes to join is

Young Judaea is a unique movement which be¬
lieves in Zionism, a healthy American Jewish com¬
munity, and the powers of youth to work for an ideal.
Today leaders, seniors, directors, and Youth Commis¬
sion members all work hand in hand triyng new ideas
and new plans for fulfilling the Young Judaea goal—
“£o develop generations of American Jewish
youth rooted in their heritage, and devoted
to attaining self-fulfillment as Zionists
In addition to the separate programs and activi¬
ties each club, city-wide functions this year included
a Ruach Rally, a discussion concerning religion in
Israel, two speakers from Israel, a raffle, and the start¬
ing of Chug Chalutzi. During the year many Judaeans
attended Winter and Summer Conventions, a District
Convention, two Chug Chalutzi Cconventions, and
many inter-city conclaves. Four Judaeans went to
Camp Tel Yehudah, the national camp of Young
Judaea, while fifteen others attended Camp Judaea in
Hendersonville, North Carolina.
During Summer Convention held at Camp Judaea
Saralee Newman was elected Programing Vice Pres¬
ident of the Southern Region, and Marvin Kliman was
elected Secretary.

’"All days of the Jewish year begin at sundown. Therefore, each
holiday really begins at sundown of the day preceding.
ROSH HASHANÁH (the Jewish New Year), a solemn two-day
holiday when the Shofar (ram's horn) sounds forth in the
synagogue to call the people to repentence and to reawaken
responsibility to the Lord.

YOM KIPPUR (Day of Atonement) is a day of fasting and prayer when we strive to reawaken the
latent spiritual power in our souls so that it may become an effective and directing force in our
daily conduct. SUKKOT (Feast of Thanksgiving) is the Jewish festival of thanksgiving on the occa¬
sion of the final ingathering of the harvest. The Sukkah symbolizes the wanderings of our ancestors
in the wilderness of Sinai.

HANUKAH (Dedication) — Anniversary of the day
when the culously,
it burned eight days.
Thus, on the first
day of Hanukah,
Jews, led by Judah Maccabee and his brothers, freed the Temple
from Syrian invaders. At the Temple's rededication, only enough
undefiled oil was found to light the Holy Lamp for one day. Mira-
Jews light one candle of the Menorah, adding another flame each
day until all eight are lit.

HAMISHA ASAR B'SHEVAT (Fifteenth Day of Shevat), the New Year of the Trees. Often referred to
as the Jewish Arbor Day, this holiday commemorates the ancient need for trees in the Holy Land. It
is celebrated everywhere with appropriate songs and games. Fruits long associated with the trees that
once grew in the Holy Land (and have begun to grow there again) are eaten on this day.

PURIM (literally, "lots") — this gayest of Jewish holidays is
nonetheless named for the lots cast before Haman to select a day
of execution for the Jews. On the fateful day, however, it was the
wicked Haman who perished — thanks to Queen Esther and her
cousin, Mordecai. The full story is told in the Megillah (Book of
Esther) which on this day, is read aloud twice in the synagogue.
During the reading, children twirl their groggers (noisemakers)
whenever Haman is mentioned, to drown out his name.

PESAH (Passover), the Feast of Unleavened Bread — an eight-day celebration of the deliverance of the Jews from
Egyptian slavery. During th traditional Seder meal, on the first two night of Passover, Jewish families read the Hag-
gadah (a ritual recitation of the story of the Exodus). Throughout the Passover periods, foods are selected according
to special dietary laws. Instead of bread, Jews eat matzos (unleavened bread) to recall the hasty flight from Egypt,
when there was not time to allow the bread to rise.

SHAVUOT — On this day, the Ten Commandments are read in the synagogue to
commemorate the giving of the Law (Torah) to Moses on Mount Sinai. Shavuot
also marks the early wheat harvest in the Holy Land. On this holiday Jews decorate
their homes and synagogues with greens and flowers.
